# Java Advanced Concepts For High Performance Development

> You’re comfortable with the basics of writing in Java, but you want to level up your skills and be more productive. In this course, Bethan Palmer has exactly what you need, with topics including generics, multi-threading, inputs and outputs, and more. After explaining the differences between versions of Java, Bethan dives into generics in Java, advanced data structures, and functional programming.

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development) | Advanced | 42K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

### 1. Getting Started

#### Which version of Java to use?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this course, I'll be using OpenJDK 17.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=4)** To be able to use all the features in this course, you will need to have at least Java 8 installed, but feel free to use any later version as well.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=11)** Choosing which Java version to use isn't completely straightforward.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=15)** Java has a 6-month release cycle, but not all Java releases are the same.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=19)** Some are known as long-term support releases and others are feature releases.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=23)** Long-term support releases are supported by Oracle for years, whereas feature releases are only supported until the next release six months later.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=31)** The current long-term support release is Java 17, and the next one will be Java 21 in September 2023.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=38)** Long-term support releases are more likely to be used by development teams.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=43)** There are now two types of JDK provided by Oracle.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=46)** One is Oracle's commercial JDK and the other is their OpenJDK.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=51)** These two different releases actually work the same and have all the same features so you could swap between them without having to make any changes to your code.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=58)** However, you do have to pay to use the Oracle JDK in production.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=62)** It's free for individual use, development, and testing, and it also comes with paid support.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=67)** Oracle's OpenJDK is completely free and is open source.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=71)** However, it doesn't come with support or updates to versions older than the current version.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=76)** As well as the two Oracle JDKs, There are also lots of versions of both the commercial JDK and OpenJDK not provided by Oracle.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=84)** Some popular versions of OpenJDK include Adoptium, Amazon Corretto, Red Hat, and several others.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=90)** I'd recommending using any one of the OpenJDK versions for this course.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/which-version-of-java-to-use?u=76281980&t=94)** I'll be using Adoptium, but it doesn't matter which one you decide to go with.

### 2. Generics in Java

#### Introduction to generics in Java
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Generics make your code safer and clearer to read.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=3)** You might have used them quite a lot already, maybe without even realizing.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=7)** But understanding how they work and knowing some of the more advanced use cases, will help you write cleaner code.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=13)** So to show why generics are needed let's have a look at an example.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=17)** In this generics example class, I have a main method and inside the main method I'm creating a new array list called shapes and I'm then adding the string circle to that array list.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=27)** So far this code works fine and I can also get that first element from the array list.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=32)** So if I do system.out.println and then pass in shapes.gets and then pass in 0 to get that first element, that will get me that first string from the list.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=42)** So to show it's working, let's run this now.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=44)** So I'm going to click on the green arrow next to the main method and choose Run.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=49)** And in the terminal, I can see it prints out the word circle as expected.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=54)** But what if I want to assign a variable to that first value from the list?
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=57)** So I'm going to add another new line and do String circle = shapes.gets and then pass in 0 to get that first element.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=69)** And I can see that straightaway I get a compiler error.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=73)** This is because the compiler doesn't know that this object is a string.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=77)** So to make it work I need to cast it.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=79)** So before the quarter shapes.gets in a pair of brackets I'm going to put the word string.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=84)** And that tells the compiler that this object is a string.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=87)** So this now compiles and will run fine.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=90)** And it might not seem like a big deal having to cast it to a string here.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=94)** But if you have a really big program where this happens lots of times and at different places in your code, it can become a big hassle.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=100)** And it's also not very readable.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=103)** It's also not very safe.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=104)** So if I look at this line where I'm creating the new array list, it's not at all obvious that this list should contain strings.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=111)** So someone else could come along later and add any different type that they'd like to this list.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=115)** So let's give that a go.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=117)** So I'm going to add another new line and I'm going to do shapes.ads.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=121)** And instead of passing in a string I'm going to pass in a rectangle type object.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=124)** So I'm going to do new Rectangle.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=129)** So this compiles fine as well.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=132)** And I can also create a new rectangle object and assign it to that second value in the list.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=136)** So I'm going to do Rectangle rectangle equals shapes.get and then pass in 1 to get that second element.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=144)** And again, I'll need to cast it.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=145)** So before shapes.get in a pair of brackets, I'm going to put the type rectangle.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=150)** So this list could contain all different types of objects and that makes it really difficult to work with.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=155)** And it also means it's easy to get it wrong when you're casting the objects.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=159)** For example, I could tell the compiler that actually instead of a rectangle, this is an integer.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=163)** So if I change rectangle to integer, and cast it to an integer, this also compiles fine.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=171)** Even though I know that I've added a rectangle object rather than an integer.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=176)** I won't know there's anything wrong until I go to run this.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=178)** So if I run it again, by clicking on this green arrow again, at this point I get a class cast exception because I'm saying it's an integer even though it's actually a rectangle.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=190)** The solution to all of these problems is generics.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=193)** So all I need to do is after the word list when I declare my list, is put a pair of angle brackets and inside the angle brackets I just need to put the type that I want this list to contain.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=202)** So in this case, I want it to contain strings.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=203)** So I'm going to put the word string.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=207)** I'm also going to put a pair of angle brackets after the arrays on the right-hand side.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=211)** But I don't need to put the type inside these ones because the compiler can infer from the left-hand side that this should contain strings.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=219)** So now I do get a compiler error when I try and add my rectangle object.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=223)** So to get this to work, I need to pass in a string.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=225)** So I'm going to change this to a string with the word rectangle in it.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=232)** And I can now get rid of all the places where I'm using my cast.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=235)** So I'm going to get rid of the cast in front of shapes.get 0 and the caste integer in front of shapes.get 1.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=241)** And I'm going to change this value from integer to string.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=246)** So generics are a powerful parts of the Java language which make our codes safer and easier for us to write and also easier for other people to read.

#### Bounded generics in Java
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Generic methods allow us to be flexible about the types that we parse in as arguments.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=5)** But sometimes you might want a middle ground between flexibility and strictness.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=9)** So in the previous video, I showed how generics can be used to let the call and order method parse in different types.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=15)** So I wrote this convert array to list method, which allows people to parse in an array containing any type of objects, and it returns a list containing the same objects of the same type.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=24)** But I might want to be a little bit stricter.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=26)** For example, I might want to only let people parse in numbers, and not allow people to parse in arrays of strings, for example.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=34)** So the solution to this problem is, bounded generics.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=37)** I can specify that only classes that extend another specific class can be used as the type.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=42)** So where I've got the T in angle brackets in my method, I can add after the T, extends number.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=49)** And that means that people will only be allowed to parse in arrays containing classes that extend the number class.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=53)** So for example, integer and double extend number.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=57)** So I can now see that I've got a compile-time error when I try and parse in my array of strings.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=63)** So let's change this array to an array of doubles.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=66)** So I'm going to change this to an array of doubles, and I'll change the name of it to doubles as well.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=73)** And instead of passing in three strings, I'll pass in three double values.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=78)** So I'll pass in just some random numbers.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=79)** So 2.0, 3.5, and 14.7.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=86)** And instead of creating a list of strings, I'm going to create a list of doubles.

> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/bounded-generics-in-java?u=76281980&t=91)** And then I can parse in my array of doubles, and that will work fine.

#### The Liskov substitution principle in Java
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The list of substitution principle helps you write more flexible and maintainable code.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980&t=5)** The name makes it sound like it's going to be something really complicated, but it's not as bad as it sounds.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980&t=10)** It just means that a subtype should behave in the same way as its supertype.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980&t=14)** So let's look at an example.

> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980&t=16)** For this chapter, I have some code that's a simple representation of an online clothing store where people can order different items of clothing.

> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980&t=23)** So first, I have an abstract class called ClothingItem and this class has two abstracts methods in it, getPrice and getName.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980&t=32)** Then there are two classes that extends this abstract class, so one of them is the ShirtItem class and in this class the getPrice method returns 10 and the getName method returns the word Shirts.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980&t=44)** Then there's one called JacketItem and in this class the getPrice method returns 25 and the getName method returns the word Jacket.

> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980&t=53)** Finally, there's a ClothingSite class.

> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980&t=56)** In here, I'm handling purchases people make on the site, so there's a method called checkoutItem, and in this method I'm printing out a sentence where it prints out the name of the item, so using the item.getName method, and then the price using the item.getPrice method.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980&t=71)** So let's have a go at using this method.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/the-liskov-substitution-principle-in-java?u=76281980&t=74)** I'm going to create a main method in this class so that I can call my checkoutItem method.

#### Using wildcards in generic programming
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the previous video, we saw how the substitution principle can help us write clean code, but it's also important to know when the principle doesn't apply.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=7)** So, in this ClothingSite class, I've got my main method and inside that I'm creating two objects, a ShirtItem and a JacketItem.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=15)** And ShirtItem and JacketItem both extends the ClothingItem class.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=19)** And then I'm creating an array list containing ClothingItems and I'm adding the shirtItem and the jacketItem to that list.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=26)** And then finally, I'm calling the checkoutAllItems method and I'm passing in my list of clothingItems.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=31)** And in the checkoutAllItems method I'm iterating over that list.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=34)** And for each one, I'm checking out the item.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=37)** But let's say, I want to create a list that just contains ShirtItems and not JacketItems.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=42)** So, I'm going to change this JacketItem to another ShirtItem, and I'll call it shirtItem2.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=51)** And on the right hand side, I'll change it to new ShirtItem.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=57)** And I need to make sure I'm passing in shirtItem2 to the list of clothingItems.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=62)** Then I'm going to change the type of the list from ClothingItem to shirtItem, because this only contains the subtype ShirtItem.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=72)** So, when I do this, I see I get a compiler error, when I call the checkoutAllItems method and pass in clothingItems.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=79)** It seems reasonable to expect that I can call this method and pass in my list of shirtItems, because shirtItem is a subtype of clothingItems.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=86)** But actually, a list of ShirtItems isn't a subtype of a list of clothingItems, even though shirtItem is a subtype of clothingItem.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=94)** So, one thing I could do is, I could write another method that takes a list of ShirtItems as an argument instead of a list of clothingItems.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=102)** But that seems like overkill to write a whole new method just to handle list of ShirtItems.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=107)** If I wanted to iterate over a list of JacketItems, I'd have to write another whole new method and I'd have to create another version of the method for any new subtypes of clothingItem I might want to add in the future.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=117)** To fix this, I can instead use wild cards.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=120)** So, in my checkoutAllItems method declaration inside the brackets, where I've got List clothingItem, before ClothingItem, I'm going to put a question mark and then the word extends and then ClothingItem.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=132)** So, this means I can now pass in a list of clothingItems, or a list containing any subtype of ClothingItem.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=138)** And I can see that my compiler error has gone now.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=140)** And if I run this just to make sure, I can see that this works as expected and it prints out the purchases of the two shirtItems.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-wildcards-in-generic-programming?u=76281980&t=153)** So, this syntax with question mark followed by extends is known as a wild card and that's how you can use wild cards in Java to make your code more flexible and save you writing a lot of unnecessary boilerplate code.

#### Solution: Using generics
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So let's have a look at the solution for the exercise for chapter two.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=9)** So the first thing I'm going to do is I'm going to uncomment the lines 25 and 26 just to see that they don't compile at the moment.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=16)** This is because the flattenList method is expecting me to pass in a list of list of strings, whereas I'm passing in a list of list of ticket types.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=24)** So I'm going to make this flattenList method a generic method.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=27)** So if I scroll down to the flattenList method, the first thing I'm going to do is before the type, I'm going to put a pair of angle brackets.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=34)** And inside that, I'm going to put a capital T to represent the type.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=38)** And instead of returning a list of strings, I'm going to return a list of type T.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=44)** And then instead of passing in a list of list of strings, I'm going to pass in a list of list of T types.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=51)** Then inside the method itself, instead of having a list of strings, I'm going to create a list of type T.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=57)** So this is now a generic method.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=59)** And if I scroll up, I can see that I no longer have a compiler error on line 25.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=65)** So the next part is to change the getTotalPrice method.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=69)** So instead of passing in a list of tickets, I want to be able to pass in either a list of tickets or a list of any subtype of tickets.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=76)** So in front of the word ticket, in the angle brackets, I'm going to put to question mark and then extends.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=83)** And now if I uncomment the rest of the lines, I can see that I don't have any compiler errors here as well.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-using-generics?u=76281980&t=92)** So if I just give this a quick run by clicking on the green arrow and choosing run, I can see that everything compiles and works fine.

### 3. Advanced Data Structures

#### Collections in Java
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Collections are a fundamental part of all object-oriented programming languages, and Java's no different.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=6)** Most programs you write will contain some kind of collection, so it's useful to have a good understanding of how they work.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=13)** A collection is an object that contains a group of other objects.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=17)** Most people are familiar with the most common data structures, like Arrays and ArrayLists, but there are, of course, many, many others, too many for me to cover here.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=25)** So in this chapter, I'm going to do some demos of how to use some particularly useful ones, including LinkedLists, HashMaps, and LinkedHashMaps.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=33)** Before we start looking at code, it's useful to understand how the collections framework works in Java.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=38)** The collections framework is a set of interfaces that represent different types of collection.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=44)** There are two main parts to the collections framework.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=46)** One part is collections and one part is maps.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=49)** One real life example equivalent of a collection is something like a shopping list.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=54)** In some collections, order is important, and in others, it's not.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=58)** And in some collections, duplicates are allowed, and in others, they're not.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=62)** A map, on the other hand, is a collection of key value pairs.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=66)** If you think about the grocery store and the stock in it, the store needs a way to track each individual item so they know what's been sold.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=73)** In this case, a big list isn't really useful, so they use barcodes.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=77)** Each unique barcode maps to a different grocery item.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=80)** So say, for example, you buy some oranges at the store.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=84)** The barcode on the bag of oranges is like the key in a map, and the bag of oranges itself is the value associated with that key.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=91)** In Java, the collections framework extends the Iterable interface.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=95)** There are no concrete classes that implement collection, but there are another set of interfaces underneath it.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=100)** So for example, Sets, List, and Queue.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=103)** Under those, there are some concrete implementations.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=106)** For example, HashSets, LinkedList, and Deque.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=109)** These are just a few examples.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=111)** There are lots of other concrete classes that extend these interfaces.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=115)** In the map section, map is the topmost interface.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=118)** There are several other interfaces that implement map.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=120)** For example, ConcurrentMap and SortedMap.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=123)** And there are several concrete classes that implement each of these.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=126)** For example, ConcurrentHashMap and TreeMap.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=130)** HashMap is a subclass of abstract HashMap, which is an abstract class that extends map.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=136)** Again, these are just a few examples of maps, there are many others not shown here.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=140)** And there's no need to memorize all these structures.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=142)** It's just useful to see the difference between collection and map.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/collections-in-java?u=76281980&t=145)** So let's have a look at some real word examples where some of these collections are used.

#### Storing pairs with HashMaps
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Sometimes you'll need a data structure that can store key-value pairs.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=4)** Probably the most common way to do this in Java is with a HashMap.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=8)** So in this example I have a class called HashmapExample and at the moment this just has an empty main method inside it.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=14)** So to create a HashMap, I'm going to put the word Hashmap and IntelliJ has added the import statement at the top for me.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=22)** And then inside the pair of angle brackets, I need to put two types.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=25)** The first type is going to be the type of the key and the second type is the type of the value associated with that key.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=31)** So let's say for example, I want to create a HashMap that represents a shopping basket in a grocery store.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=37)** So the key is going to be the name of the product being scanned and the value is going to be the number of times that item has been scanned.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=43)** So for example, if someone scans one apple, the key will be apple and the value will be one.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=49)** Then if they scan another apple, the key will still be apple but the value will be incremented to two.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=55)** So in this case, the key is going to be a string to represent the name of the product, and then the value is going to be an integer to represent how many times it's been scanned.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=64)** So I'll call this basket, so I can do basket equals new HashMap.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=70)** So the method to add items to a HashMap is called put.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=74)** So let's say, for example, I want to add an apple to my basket.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=77)** So I can do basket.put and then I'm going to pass in the string apple and then the value associated with it is going to be one.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=86)** There's also a handy method to get the value associated with a key.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=90)** So for example, if I want to get the value associated with apple, I can do basket.get and pass in apple.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=96)** So I'm going to do system.out.println and I'm going to pass in basket.get and then pass in the key apple.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=104)** So this should return the value one.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=108)** Let's run this now and in the terminal, I can see that I've got one as expected.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=116)** Let's see what happens if we update an existing key in a map.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=119)** So this time, I'm going to do basket.put again and I'm going to pass in Apple again but this time I'm going to pass in the value two.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=129)** Then I'm going to copy and paste this line where I do system.out.println and pass in basket.get and pass in apple to get the value associated with the key apple.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=138)** And if I run this again, I can see that this time I get the value two.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=143)** So keys inside HashMaps are unique.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=145)** So the second time I did basket.put and passed in apple, it didn't create a new key for apple, it just updated the existing one.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=153)** Another useful method is the contains key method, which I can use to check if a key is already being used inside the HashMap.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=159)** So I'm going to do system.out.println and this time I'm going to pass in basket.containsKey and then pass in the key apple.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=167)** So this should return true because I know that my HashMap has a key called apple in it.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=172)** So if I run this again, I can see that I get the value true.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=179)** One more method that I've personally found really useful when working with HashMaps is a method called merge.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=185)** So let's say for my shopping basket application, I want to increment the value of a product each time it's scanned rather than setting it manually.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=192)** So to do that, I'd have to check if the key is already used inside the basket using the containsKey method.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=199)** Then if it is, I would need to get the current value using the get method and then increment that value by one.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=206)** And then I would have to call the put method again and pass in the key and then the new value again.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=212)** And then if the key isn't already inside the basket, I would have to do another put and then pass in one.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=220)** So the merge method actually handles all of this logic for us really simply.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=226)** If I do basket.merge, this method takes three arguments.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=232)** So the first one is the key and that's going to be the name of the product in this case.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=235)** So I can pass in apple.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=239)** The second argument is what I want the value to be if the key isn't already inside the map.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=243)** So if it's the first time apple has been scanned, I want it to be one.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=248)** Then the third argument is a remapping function and the result of that function is used if the product already exists.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=254)** So here to increment the value by one, I can do integer followed by two colons, followed by sum.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=261)** So this whole line is saying if apple is already inside the map, increment the value by one.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=266)** If it's not, then add a new key for apple and set the value to one.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=271)** So let's also try this method with a key that we know isn't already inside the map.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=275)** So I'm going to do basket.merge and this time I'm going to pass in orange.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=280)** And then I want the value to be one if there are no oranges in the basket.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=283)** And then I'm going to do integer::sum to say that if there are already oranges in the basket, add one to the value.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=291)** So for the apple one, the value should now be three because I'm adding one to the two that are already in the basket.

> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=297)** And for the orange one, the value should be one.

> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/storing-pairs-with-hashmaps?u=76281980&t=309)** So if I run this again, I can see that for this one I get three for apple and I get one for orange.

#### Using LinkedHashMaps
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=0)** - In the previous video in this chapter, we looked at HashMaps and LinkedLists.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=5)** As you might be able to guess, LinkedHashMaps are sort of a combination of those two things.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=10)** So in the previous video, I used an example of a store where you can scan items, and the item is added to a HashMap as the key, and the value is the number of times it's been scanned.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=19)** So similarly here I've got a HashMap called basket and I'm adding two apples, one orange and three bananas to the HashMap.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=27)** And then I'm calling a method called forEach on the HashMap, and this prints out the key and the value for each of the items in the basket.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=34)** I'll explain more about how this lambda index works in the next chapter.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=38)** So if I run this now, I can see that I get my three elements in the baskets, but they're in a completely random order.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=46)** So I put apple, orange, and banana, but when I print them out, I get orange first, then banana, and then apple.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=53)** So this shows that HashMaps don't maintain any order, it's just completely random.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=57)** But in some situations you might want to keep some order in your HashMap.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=61)** And the way to do that is to use a LinkedHashmap rather than a HashMap.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=65)** So I'm going to replace my HashMap with a LinkedHashmap, and I'll do the same on the right hand side of the assignments.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=74)** IntelliJ has added the LinkedHashmap import statement for me, as I'm going to get rid of this old HashMap import statements that I'm not using anymore.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=82)** So if I run this again now, I can see that this time, I get apple and then orange and banana.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=91)** So by default, the order for LinkedHashmap is the order that the elements were inserted into the map.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=96)** But it's also possible to order them by when they were last accessed.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=100)** So to do this, I need to use a different constructor for the LinkedHashmap.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=104)** So I need to pass in three arguments.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=106)** The first one is going to be the initial capacity which means how many items I can put in the map when it's first created before it gets resized.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=113)** So I'll give this 10 for now, which is plenty of space for what I need.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=118)** The second one is called the load factor which is how full it can get before it's made bigger.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=123)** So I'm going to put 0.75f, which is the default value anyway.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=128)** And the third argument is the one we're actually interested in here, which is whether to use access order, so I'll set that to true.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=135)** So this means that it will return the least recently accessed element first.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=142)** So if I run this again, I actually get everything back in the same order because apple was the least recently accessed.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=150)** But if I access one of the elements after adding it, it will change the order.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=153)** So if I add another line where I say basket.get and pass in apple, this should put apple to the bottom of the list because it's now the most recently accessed.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=164)** So if I rerun this, I can see that apple is now at the bottom of the list.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=171)** It's also worth noting that the implementation of LinkedHashmap is based on both LinkedList and HashMap, but the interface is only based on HashMap.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-linkedhashmaps?u=76281980&t=179)** So if you wanted to use some of LinkedList methods, like getFirst and getLast, these aren't actually available for LinkedHashmap.

#### Solution: Data structures
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-data-structures?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-data-structures?u=76281980&t=0)** (upbeat synth music) - [Instructor] So let's go through the solution for the voting system exercise.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-data-structures?u=76281980&t=8)** The first step is to create a linked hash map field.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-data-structures?u=76281980&t=12)** So under this comment, I'm going to create a private field and I'm going to say linked hash map.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/solution-data-structures?u=76281980&t=17)** And then inside the angle brackets I put my two types.
>

#### Using method references in Java
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Another feature that was introduced in Java eight is method references.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=4)** These are another type of lambda expression and they're another way of making your code more concise.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=10)** There are actually four different types of method references.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=13)** One of the types is used when you're calling a static method.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=15)** So let's have a look at that type first.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=18)** In this example I have a class called number utils and inside here I have a single static method called even or odd.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=25)** This method takes an INT as an argument and it does a calculation's work out if the number is even or odd.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=32)** If it's even, it prints out a message saying that the number is even, and if not it prints out a message saying that the number is odd.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=39)** So I've also got a main class in here where I've got a list containing the numbers one to seven.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=44)** So let's say I want to iterate over this list and for each number I want to call the even or odd static method from the number utils class.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=52)** So to do this, I'm going to use another very useful method which was introduced in Java eight called forEach, which lets you iterate over a collection.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=60)** So I'm going to do numbers dots forEach and to use this method, I need to pass in something called a consumer.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=67)** So if I hover over the brackets and I click on consumer IntelliJ gives me some of the Java doc for this class.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=75)** So the first thing I see is I see that this is a functional interface.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=80)** And it says that it represents an operation that accepts a single input argument and returns no results.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=86)** So in the previous video I looked at how we can implement a functional interface with the lander.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=90)** So I'm going to do that here inside the forEach method.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=94)** So first I have a pair of brackets where I pass in any arguments.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=100)** This does take an argument and in this case it's going to be the item that I'm iterating over.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=104)** So I'm going to call it number.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=106)** Note that I don't need to put the type here because this will be inferred by the compiler.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=111)** Then I have my arrow so a dash and then a right angle bracket.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=115)** And then I have the method body.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=117)** So here I'm going to call the number utils method even or odd and pass in the number.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=121)** So I'm going to do number utils dot even or odd and pass in number.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=126)** So this code will iterate over the numbers list and for each one print out if it's even or odd.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=131)** So if I run this now, I can see down in the terminal that I get, one is odd two is even, three is odd, and so on.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=140)** However, we can actually shorten this lambda by using a method reference instead.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=145)** All this lambda is a actually doing is it's just calling another method.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=149)** This even or odd, a static method.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=151)** So I can shorten this by actually getting rid of all of the lambda syntax.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=155)** So I'm going to get rid of the number in brackets and the arrow.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=158)** And I'm going to get rid of the word number in brackets so that I'm just left with number util dots even or odd.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=164)** And I'm going to replace the dots with two colons.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=168)** So this is a shorter way of writing the exact same functionality.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=172)** And if I run this again, I can see that I still get the same outputs as before.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=178)** So that's how method references can help you write even more concise code while still keeping it readable.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=183)** Here we're just left with the actual method being called on each iteration without the slightly more confusing lambda syntax.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-method-references-in-java?u=76281980&t=190)** So here I was calling the static method even or odd but you can also use this method reference syntax when calling an instance method of an object or the constructor of an object.
