#### Understanding streams in Java
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Streams provide a powerful way to work with sequences of elements in Java.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=5)** They have a slightly confusing name and that they sound similar to input and output streams, but actually, they're completely different things.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=12)** The most common way to create a stream using the Streams API is from an existing collection.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=17)** So for example, if I have a list of integers called numbers, I could turn that into a stream by doing numbers.stream to call the stream method, and that would give me back a stream.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=27)** There are two different types of operation you can perform on a stream, intermediate and terminal.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=32)** Intermediate operations always return another stream so you can chain them together.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=37)** Terminal operations return void or any other type that's not a stream, so you can't call anything else once you've called a terminal operation.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=45)** One example of an intermediate method is sorted.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=47)** So if I call the sorted method on my stream of integers, this would sort the numbers from lowest to highest.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=53)** And because this is an intermediate operation, it returns a new stream.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=57)** Note that it doesn't return a collection.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=60)** A stream is not the same as a collection.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=62)** A collection stores data and you can iterate over it as many times as you want.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=66)** Streams are just used to perform operations on the data and don't actually store it.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=71)** An example of a terminal operation is forEach, which can be used to perform an operation on each element in the stream.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=78)** So for example, I could print out each element.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-streams-in-java?u=76281980&t=81)** Let's have a further look at some real-life examples of streams.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (3), pass (3), lambda (2), class, (1), static (1)

### 5. Concurrency in Java

#### Introduction to concurrency in Java
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Making use of concurrency in Java can help you write faster applications by letting your code do more than one thing at a time.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=7)** As an analogy, imagine a chef is cooking a meal, and as part of the meal, they need to cook both rice and potatoes.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=13)** If they had a stove that only had one burner they'd have to cook them one at a time which would be quite slow.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=19)** If they had two burners, they'd be able to half the time it takes to cook the food by cooking the rice and potatoes at the same time.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=25)** Similarly, with coding, if you have two threads running at the same time, your program can do two things together which can make it run faster.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=34)** An example of when threads can be useful in Java is when you have blocking input and output functions.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=39)** For example, if the program asks the user to input some data, a read method might be used which blocks the program until the user has input something.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=46)** This might prevent the program from performing other tasks while waiting for the user.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=51)** If they try to do something else, like click on a button, nothing would happen.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=55)** If the program has a graphical user interface, or GUI for short, multi-threading can be used to improve the responsiveness of the program and stop it from hanging.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=64)** In particular, if there is a large and complex program that performs independent tasks, using multi-threading is often necessary.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=72)** So using multiple threads can make software run faster.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=75)** But what actually is a thread?
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=77)** A thread is a lightweight process.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=80)** In programming a single process can have multiple threads working at the same time.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=84)** Like a process, a thread is an independent path of execution that runs in isolation.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=89)** Each thread has its own stack and its own local variables.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=92)** So when a method is running on a thread, the local variables in the method are only available within that thread.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=99)** This leads to the question, "Why not just use multiple processes instead of multiple threads?"
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=104)** One reason is that creating a new process uses much more resources.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=108)** Threads are more closely connected to each other than separate processes are.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=112)** When you open an email app on your computer, and then open a browser window, those things are separate processes and have nothing to do with each other.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=119)** But threads inside the same process are more closely connected.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=122)** They share memory with other threads.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=124)** This means that they can communicate with each other.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=128)** All of the threads have the same access to global variables.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=131)** This can sometimes lead to issues and means that care should be taken when using multiple threads.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=136)** Java has its own API for handling threads which is actually quite simple to use, but care should be taken when using threads in complex applications.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=144)** Multiple threads that perform operations on the same data need particular care.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/introduction-to-concurrency-in-java?u=76281980&t=149)** Common problems include deadlock and livelock, for example.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Code Keywords:** else, (1), interface (1)

> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-the-executorservice-in-java?u=76281980&t=4)** However, in a real production system, it's unlikely that we'll want to specifically create individual threads like this.

> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-the-executorservice-in-java?u=76281980&t=11)** Here it's quite easy to keep track of the different threads, because there's only two of them, but in a large application, it does get more difficult.

> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-the-executorservice-in-java?u=76281980&t=185)** And I didn't have to worry about configuring the stopping and starting and reusing of threads, as the ExecutorService handles all of those things, in the way that I tell it to.

#### Synchronized methods in Java
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you're working with threads, there are some things that can go wrong that is worth keeping an eye out for.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=5)** So in this example, I have a class that represents a bank account.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=9)** This class has a field called balance, which is set to 100, and then it has a method called debit, which takes an amount as an argument and then it subtracts that amount from the balance.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=19)** And then there's a method called getBalance, which returns the balance.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=23)** There's also a class called ATM, which has a withdraw method.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=28)** So this method takes an account and an amount as arguments, and the first thing it does is it gets the balance of the bank account.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=35)** Then it checks to see if minusing the amount from the balance would take the balance below zero.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=40)** If it does, it prints out a message saying, "Transaction denied."
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=44)** If it doesn't, then it prints out a message saying, "Handling transaction," and then it calls the debit method on the account to minus the amount from the balance.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=51)** And then it prints out a message saying how much money was withdrawn.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=55)** And then finally, after the if-else statement, it prints out a message saying what the current balance is, using the account.getBalance method.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=62)** Finally, I have a class called Customer.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=65)** So this represents a person using an ATM to withdraw money.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=70)** In here, I have a main method where I'm creating an ATM object and a BankAccount object.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=76)** Imagine a scenario where an account is shared by two people and there are two cards associated with that account.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=82)** It's possible that both people will try and withdraw money at the same time.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=85)** So to represent this, I have an executor service with a fixed thread pool of two.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=91)** Then I'm submitting two tasks to the service.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=93)** So that represents two people trying to withdraw $100 from the same account at the same time.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=99)** Then I'm calling the shutdown method on the executor service.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=103)** So the balance of the account shouldn't go below zero.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=106)** So we're interested to see what happens when two people try and withdraw $100 from the account at the same time.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=113)** So if I run this, I can see that something odd has happened.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=117)** Both people have been able to withdraw $100.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=120)** So that takes the balance to -100, which shouldn't be allowed to happen.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=125)** Let's have a look at the withdraw method in the ATM class to see why this might have happened.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=129)** So if I go back to the ATM class, what might have happened is the first thread might have gone into this method and then it would call the getBalance method on the account, which should return $100.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=140)** Then it checks if balance minus amount is less than zero.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=143)** So 100 minus 100 equals zero, so that's not less than zero.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=147)** It would go onto this else statement.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=150)** And then at this point, the second thread might enter the method.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=153)** And this thread will also call the getBalance method on the account, and that will also return 100.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=158)** So when the second thread checks if balance minus amount is less than zero, it will also return false and go onto this else statement.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=165)** So both threads will go inside this else statement and debit the account.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=170)** This seems like it might be quite a complex problem to solve.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=173)** But in this case, there is actually quite a simple solution.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=176)** I can make this withdraw method a synchronized method.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=179)** And I can do this by putting the word synchronized before the return type.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=182)** So after public and before void, I'm going to put the word synchronized.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=187)** This means that only one thread will be allowed to enter this method at a time.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=191)** So if I go back to my Customer class, if I rerun this, I can guarantee that this time the account balance won't go below zero.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/synchronized-methods-in-java?u=76281980&t=200)** So if I run it a few times just to make sure, I can see that I consistently get the balance as zero and I never get the error where it goes down to -100.

### 6. Input and Output

#### Understanding I/O in Java
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Inputs and OutputStreams are a way of reading data or writing data.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=4)** There are lots of different use cases for using Streams and they're used more often than you might think.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=9)** Even a simple "Hello World" application uses them.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=12)** When you have a System.out.println statement, that is actually using an OutputStream to print out the text.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=18)** Streams are most often used for things like reading from and writing to files and taking user inputs from the console.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=25)** Streams represent a flow of data, and a bit like streams of water, they can only go in one direction.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=31)** OutputStreams write out data and InputStreams read in data.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=35)** If you wanted to have data going two ways, you would need two separate Streams.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=39)** There are two core abstract classes in the Java Streams API, InputStream and OutputStream.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=45)** As these are abstract classes, you can't instantiate them directly, but they each have several concrete implementations for handling different types of data.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=53)** For example, concrete implementations of InputStream include FileInputStream for reading files, and ByteArrayInputStream for reading bytes and so on.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=61)** Similarly, implementations of OutputStream include FileOutputStream and ByteArrayOutputStream for writing out files and byte arrays respectively.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=71)** There are two more abstract classes called Reader and Writer.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=74)** These are similar to Input and OutputStream and they are built on the same concepts, but the main difference is that InputStream and OutputStream move bytes around, whereas Reader and Writer move characters around.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=85)** Characters are easier and more intuitive to work with than bytes.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=88)** They can handle Unicode characters and other character encoding issues, which byte streams don't.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/understanding-i-o-in-java?u=76281980&t=94)** Again, these are abstract classes, so it's the concrete implementations that are used depending on the type of data that you are handling.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** abstract (4)
> **Analogies:** for example (1), similar to (1)
> **Env Vars:** api (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-from-system-in-with-a-scanner?u=76281980&t=179)** The nextLine method will consume the line separator at the end of the line and then move on to the next line.

> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-from-system-in-with-a-scanner?u=76281980&t=190)** So after we've read the integer, the scanner returns the new line character and doesn't move on to the next line.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-from-system-in-with-a-scanner?u=76281980&t=197)** So when I say scanner.nextLine, it just returns the new line character.

> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-from-system-in-with-a-scanner?u=76281980&t=202)** There are a couple of solutions to this problem.

> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-from-system-in-with-a-scanner?u=76281980&t=204)** One is instead of using nextInt here, I could actually change this to use nextLine and then convert the string to an integer.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-from-system-in-with-a-scanner?u=76281980&t=212)** Another option is to add another call to nextLine after this call to nextInt so that the scanner moves on past the line break.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-from-system-in-with-a-scanner?u=76281980&t=219)** So I'm going to add another line and I'm going to say scanner.nextLine.

> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-files-with-bufferedreader?u=76281980&t=250)** So I'm going to add another system.out.println.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-files-with-bufferedreader?u=76281980&t=253)** And this time I'm going to print out a message that says rest of the lines.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-files-with-bufferedreader?u=76281980&t=259)** And then I'm going to have a system.out.println and pass in at my stringBuilder.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-files-with-bufferedreader?u=76281980&t=265)** So if I run this again now, I can see I get my first line and then I have the rest of the lines as one very long string.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-files-with-bufferedreader?u=76281980&t=276)** And it's got that space in between each line as well.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-files-with-bufferedreader?u=76281980&t=280)** Note that when I call this lines method, it doesn't actually return the first line.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/reading-files-with-bufferedreader?u=76281980&t=283)** So you can't see the first line at the beginning of the rest of the lines output.

#### Using try-with-resources with I/O
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Here we have the example I showed in the previous video of how I can read a file with a buffered reader.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=5)** So if I run this main method, it prints out the first line from a file, and then all of the other lines as one long string.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=14)** However, there are a couple of things I can improve in this code.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=17)** One thing that I don't do is I don't close the reader.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=21)** If you open resources like this without closing them, they will use up operating system resources.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=26)** For example, if you leave a database connection open, it may lead to a limit on the resources being reached, and the application server having to keep restarting.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=35)** One way to handle this is to add a finally block after the try cath blocks, to make sure the resource is closed after it's been used.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=42)** However, if you have a large application where you are opening several resources in different places, it can be quite easy to miss this part.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=49)** Luckily, there is a simpler way to handle this scenario called, try-with-resources.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=54)** The syntax for try-with-resources is to declare the resource inside brackets after the word try.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=60)** So after the word try, I'm going to put a pair of brackets.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=63)** And then I'm actually going to cut this line, and I'm going to paste it inside the brackets.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=73)** So if I run the app again now, I can see that I get the same output as before.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=80)** So this is quite a small change, but it can make your application safer by preventing memory leaks.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/using-try-with-resources-with-i-o?u=76281980&t=85)** So you should use try-with-resources wherever you are creating resources inside a try block.

### 7. Working with Files and Directories

#### Working with files in Java
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Java has always had ways to handle files right from the very first version.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=5)** There are two different packages that contain classes that we can use to work with files, [java.io](https://java.io) and java.nio.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=11)** There is a class called file in the [java.io](https://java.io) package and that can be used to do things like create new files and list files in a directory.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=19)** This class has been around since the beginning, and it's unlikely to be deprecated because it's been used so much, but it does have a few drawbacks.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=27)** These drawbacks are addressed by the classes in the java.nio package.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=32)** This introduced some new classes such as Path, Paths, and Files.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=36)** These classes give us a newer API that can do everything the file class can do but has better support for things like error handling.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=42)** So in general, it's a better option to go with the classes in java.nio for working with files.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=48)** And because of these improvements, I'll be using the classes in the java.nio package in my examples, but you might see the file class used in older code examples.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=57)** Two of the main classes I'll be using from the java.nio package are Path and Paths.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=62)** A Path object represents a path on the file system, and the Paths class provides a static method called Get, which is used to convert strings to Path objects for us.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=71)** Then there's the Files class, not to be confused with the file class in the [java.io](https://java.io) package.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/java-advanced-concepts-for-high-performance-development/working-with-files-in-java?u=76281980&t=76)** This provides several static methods that can be used to work with files, such as copying, moving, and deleting files.

> **Env Vars:** api (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
